(function(win, doc, style, timeout) {
    var STYLE_ID = 'at-body-style';
  
    function getParent() {
      return doc.getElementsByTagName('head')[0];
    }
  
    function addStyle(parent, id, def) {
      if (!parent) {
        return;
      }
  
      var style = doc.createElement('style');
      style.id = id;
      style.innerHTML = def;
      parent.appendChild(style);
    }
  
    function removeStyle(parent, id) {
      if (!parent) {
        return;
      }
  
      var style = doc.getElementById(id);
  
      if (!style) {
        return;
      }
  
      parent.removeChild(style);
    }

    function initFunc() {
        addStyle(getParent(), STYLE_ID, style);
        setTimeout(function() {
            removeStyle(getParent(), STYLE_ID);
        }, timeout);
    }

      }
      return null;
  }

<div class="cmp-title" id="title-2c9652cd8c" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-2c9652cd8c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Spoofing DNS Records by Abusing DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates | Akamai&#34;}}">
    
      <h1 class="cmp-title__text">Spoofing DNS Records by Abusing DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates</h1>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="section section__vertical-center aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

  
  
  
      

      

  <div class="cmp-section   " data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;section-1a759aab5d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/section&#34;}}">
    
    
  
    
      <div class="cmp-section__row 1 cmp-section__2-columns">
        <div class="cmp-section__row-wrapper cmp-section__two-column--separate ">

<div class="text text__pt-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-d9cd2c5af4&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T17:21:05Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Executive summary&lt;/h2>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Akamai researchers discovered a new set of attacks against Active Directory domains that use Microsoft Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol (DHCP) servers.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>These attacks could allow attackers to spoof sensitive DNS records, resulting in varying consequences from credential theft to full Active Directory domain compromise. The attacks don&#39;t require any credentials, and work with the default configuration of Microsoft DHCP server.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The number of impacted organizations can be significant. Microsoft DHCP server is very popular; it was observed running in 40% of the networks monitored by Akamai.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>We reported our findings to Microsoft, but a fix is not planned.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>In this blog post, we &lt;a href=\&#34;#mitigation\&#34; target=\&#34;_self\&#34;>detail the best practices for configuring Microsoft DHCP server&lt;/a> in a way that mitigates these attacks, and &lt;a href=\&#34;#detecting\&#34; target=\&#34;_self\&#34;>share a tool&lt;/a> meant to be used by system administrators and blue teams to detect risky configurations.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>In a future blog post, we will share technical details about the implementation of these attacks from an attacker’s point of view.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Executive summary</h2>
<ul>
<li><p>Akamai researchers discovered a new set of attacks against Active Directory domains that use Microsoft Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol (DHCP) servers. </p>
</li>
<li><p>These attacks could allow attackers to spoof sensitive DNS records, resulting in varying consequences from credential theft to full Active Directory domain compromise. The attacks don't require any credentials, and work with the default configuration of Microsoft DHCP server.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The number of impacted organizations can be significant. Microsoft DHCP server is very popular; it was observed running in 40% of the networks monitored by Akamai.</p>
</li>
<li><p>We reported our findings to Microsoft, but a fix is not planned.</p>
</li>
<li><p>In this blog post, we <a href="#mitigation" target="_self">detail the best practices for configuring Microsoft DHCP server</a> in a way that mitigates these attacks, and <a href="#detecting" target="_self">share a tool</a> meant to be used by system administrators and blue teams to detect risky configurations.</p>
</li>
<li><p>In a future blog post, we will share technical details about the implementation of these attacks from an attacker’s point of view.</p>
</li>
</ul>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-1d180706d2&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T13:56:03Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Introduction&lt;/h2>\r\n&lt;p>The ability to spoof DNS records is very appealing to attackers, as it can lead to devastating consequences, including sensitive data exposure, credential compromise, and even remote code execution.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>In this blog post, we examine an attack surface in DNS that has been rarely researched, and is exposed by a seemingly harmless DHCP feature. By using it, we found several different ways that attackers could spoof DNS records on Microsoft DNS servers, &lt;b>including an unauthenticated arbitrary DNS record overwrite.&lt;/b>&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Alongside the attack flows, we also describe in detail the inner workings of a Microsoft DHCP server, its interaction with DNS and Active Directory, and how to properly secure these interfaces. Although many scattered (and inaccurate!) resources on DHCP exist online, we believe this blog post to be an accurate, comprehensive resource on the subject where all the critical information for defenders is presented in one place.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Introduction</h2>
<p>The ability to spoof DNS records is very appealing to attackers, as it can lead to devastating consequences, including sensitive data exposure, credential compromise, and even remote code execution.</p>
<p>In this blog post, we examine an attack surface in DNS that has been rarely researched, and is exposed by a seemingly harmless DHCP feature. By using it, we found several different ways that attackers could spoof DNS records on Microsoft DNS servers, <b>including an unauthenticated arbitrary DNS record overwrite.</b></p>
<p>Alongside the attack flows, we also describe in detail the inner workings of a Microsoft DHCP server, its interaction with DNS and Active Directory, and how to properly secure these interfaces. Although many scattered (and inaccurate!) resources on DHCP exist online, we believe this blog post to be an accurate, comprehensive resource on the subject where all the critical information for defenders is presented in one place.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" id="dns" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;dns&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T21:22:49Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>DNS and Active Directory&lt;/h2>\r\n&lt;p>Our journey begins with Active Directory (AD). AD heavily relies on DNS for its operation. Every domain needs a DNS server that hosts a special DNS zone called Active Directory Integrated DNS (ADIDNS) zone (Figure 1). This zone is used to host DNS records for all domain-joined machines and the different services in AD.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>DNS and Active Directory</h2>
<p>Our journey begins with Active Directory (AD). AD heavily relies on DNS for its operation. Every domain needs a DNS server that hosts a special DNS zone called Active Directory Integrated DNS (ADIDNS) zone (Figure 1). This zone is used to host DNS records for all domain-joined machines and the different services in AD.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Every domain needs a DNS server that hosts a special DNS zone called Active Directory Integrated DNS (ADIDNS) zone (Figure 1)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-55189050a9&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:26:32Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Every domain needs a DNS server that hosts a special DNS zone called Active Directory Integrated DNS (ADIDNS) zone (Figure 1).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;b330072d-6b3f-4544-a9c9-b20ad75fd337&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:25:34Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-1.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-1.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="Every domain needs a DNS server that hosts a special DNS zone called Active Directory Integrated DNS (ADIDNS) zone (Figure 1)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 1: The default ADIDNS</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-d2d95c2bd4&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:27:00Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Records in ADI zones are managed using a DNS feature called &lt;i>Dynamic Updates&lt;/i>. This feature allows each client to be responsible for its own record — when they need to create or modify their DNS record, they send a special DNS request that includes the data that needs to be modified on the server (Figure 2). When the DNS server receives this request, it modifies the client’s records accordingly.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Records in ADI zones are managed using a DNS feature called <i>Dynamic Updates</i>. This feature allows each client to be responsible for its own record — when they need to create or modify their DNS record, they send a special DNS request that includes the data that needs to be modified on the server (Figure 2). When the DNS server receives this request, it modifies the client’s records accordingly.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="container responsivegrid cmp-container--paddingLeftMedium cmp-container--paddingRightMedium aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

    
    
    
   

    

          <div data-scroll-to-id="container-ffe42ee704" class="cmp-container ">
          
               <div class="image">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title=" This feature allows each client to be responsible for its own record — when they need to create or modify their DNS record, they send a special DNS request that includes the data that needs to be modified on the server (Figure 2). " class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-3e68192316&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:30:59Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;This feature allows each client to be responsible for its own record — when they need to create or modify their DNS record, they send a special DNS request that includes the data that needs to be modified on the server (Figure 2).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;3920f998-54e5-4f0e-a27d-919cc8d5b7e6&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:28:51Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-2.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-2.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt=" This feature allows each client to be responsible for its own record — when they need to create or modify their DNS record, they send a special DNS request that includes the data that needs to be modified on the server (Figure 2). "/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 2: An example of a DNS update’s content</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>

               
          </div>

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-6ffc576b6d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:30:49Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>One of the important features of DNS Dynamic Updates is &lt;i>Secure Updates&lt;/i>, which is designed&amp;nbsp; to control who can modify each DNS record in the zone. Without secure updates, the DNS server blindly obeys any update request, which allows attackers to easily overwrite existing records. With this feature, only secure updates are accepted by the DNS server for ADI zones by default (Figure 3).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>One of the important features of DNS Dynamic Updates is <i>Secure Updates</i>, which is designed  to control who can modify each DNS record in the zone. Without secure updates, the DNS server blindly obeys any update request, which allows attackers to easily overwrite existing records. With this feature, only secure updates are accepted by the DNS server for ADI zones by default (Figure 3).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="container responsivegrid cmp-container--paddingLeftMedium cmp-container--paddingRightMedium aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

    
    
    
   

    

          <div data-scroll-to-id="container-ed4a25fa70" class="cmp-container ">
          
               <div class="image">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="With this feature, only secure updates are accepted by the DNS server for ADI zones by default (Figure 3)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-52846397d4&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:32:41Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;With this feature, only secure updates are accepted by the DNS server for ADI zones by default (Figure 3).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;801cf36b-6a01-421c-bf6c-0bd66c822e8a&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:32:32Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-3.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-3.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="With this feature, only secure updates are accepted by the DNS server for ADI zones by default (Figure 3)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 3: DNS Dynamic Updates default settings</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

          </div>

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b6e86da101&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:33:31Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>&lt;b>When Secure Updates are used, all update requests received by the server are authenticated and authorized&lt;/b> — in ADI zones, this is performed using Kerberos. When an update is sent to the server, it includes a Kerberos ticket that is used to authenticate the user (Figure 4). For more information on the Kerberos authentication process over DNS, refer to Dirk-Jan Mollema’s research on &lt;a href=\&#34;https://dirkjanm.io/relaying-kerberos-over-dns-with-krbrelayx-and-mitm6/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>relaying Kerberos over DNS&lt;/a>.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p><b>When Secure Updates are used, all update requests received by the server are authenticated and authorized</b> — in ADI zones, this is performed using Kerberos. When an update is sent to the server, it includes a Kerberos ticket that is used to authenticate the user (Figure 4). For more information on the Kerberos authentication process over DNS, refer to Dirk-Jan Mollema’s research on <a href="https://dirkjanm.io/relaying-kerberos-over-dns-with-krbrelayx-and-mitm6/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">relaying Kerberos over DNS</a>.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="When an update is sent to the server, it includes a Kerberos ticket that is used to authenticate the user (Figure 4)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-39f803218b&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:34:32Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;When an update is sent to the server, it includes a Kerberos ticket that is used to authenticate the user (Figure 4).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;742f5c4e-cca4-41c0-9d84-27b55c2df7ce&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:34:23Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-4.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-4.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="When an update is sent to the server, it includes a Kerberos ticket that is used to authenticate the user (Figure 4)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 4: A Kerberos ticket inside a DNS update</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-4f0c784318&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:35:06Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Each DNS record is protected by an access control list (ACL) that determines the access rights for each principal. These access rights are determined when the record is initially created: When a client creates a DNS record by sending a DNS Dynamic Update, the account of the machine that created the DNS record is automatically assigned as the record’s owner, and is given permissions over it. Normally, every DNS client uses its own machine account ticket to perform DNS updates. To authorize an update request, the DNS server verifies the record’s ACL against the authenticated principal.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>In Figure 5, we can see the ACL for the DNS record of the host “&lt;i>PC.aka.test&lt;/i>”. This record was created by the computer account, so it has permissions to modify it.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Each DNS record is protected by an access control list (ACL) that determines the access rights for each principal. These access rights are determined when the record is initially created: When a client creates a DNS record by sending a DNS Dynamic Update, the account of the machine that created the DNS record is automatically assigned as the record’s owner, and is given permissions over it. Normally, every DNS client uses its own machine account ticket to perform DNS updates. To authorize an update request, the DNS server verifies the record’s ACL against the authenticated principal.</p>
<p>In Figure 5, we can see the ACL for the DNS record of the host “<i>PC.aka.test</i>”. This record was created by the computer account, so it has permissions to modify it.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

          </div>

<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-f8878e2cc3&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:37:50Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Other principals (except some built-in strong groups) shouldn’t have permissions over the record. When a different principal attempts to modify a DNS record that they don’t own or have permissions over, the server refuses the update.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>ADIDNS zones can be very interesting for attackers. &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.netspi.com/blog/technical/network-penetration-testing/exploiting-adidns/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Previous research by Kevin Robertson&lt;/a> of NETSPI highlighted some interesting attacks on these DNS zones. We wanted to expand on this attack surface, so we began digging into additional related features, which led us to an interesting one — DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Other principals (except some built-in strong groups) shouldn’t have permissions over the record. When a different principal attempts to modify a DNS record that they don’t own or have permissions over, the server refuses the update.</p>
<p>ADIDNS zones can be very interesting for attackers. <a href="https://www.netspi.com/blog/technical/network-penetration-testing/exploiting-adidns/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Previous research by Kevin Robertson</a> of NETSPI highlighted some interesting attacks on these DNS zones. We wanted to expand on this attack surface, so we began digging into additional related features, which led us to an interesting one — DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-25d65fbd61&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T21:21:40Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates&lt;/h2>\r\n&lt;p>DHCP is a network management protocol used to automatically assign IP addresses and other network options to clients. When a client joins a new network they will try to reach a DHCP server by sending a broadcast message, requesting network configurations. When a DHCP server receives this request, it will respond to the client with an assigned IP address.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>DHCP is a very common protocol that is used in most corporate networks, and the Microsoft DHCP server, in particular, is a very popular option — &lt;b>we saw the Microsoft DHCP server running on 40% of the data center networks we monitor.&lt;/b>&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Although modern Windows clients (&lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.itprotoday.com/compute-engines/dynamic-dns-updates-windows-2000\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Windows 2000 and above&lt;/a>) normally create their own records by sending DNS Dynamic Updates, this is not always the case. DNS records can also be created using a DHCP feature called &lt;i>DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates&lt;/i>. &lt;b>The purpose of this feature is to allow a DHCP server to register DNS records on behalf of its clients&lt;/b>. Whenever a client is given an IP address by the DHCP server, the latter can contact the DNS server and update the client’s DNS record. To perform these updates, the DHCP server uses (surprise, surprise!) DNS Dynamic Updates.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The name similarity can be quite confusing, so let&#39;s clarify:&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates</h2>
<p>DHCP is a network management protocol used to automatically assign IP addresses and other network options to clients. When a client joins a new network they will try to reach a DHCP server by sending a broadcast message, requesting network configurations. When a DHCP server receives this request, it will respond to the client with an assigned IP address.</p>
<p>DHCP is a very common protocol that is used in most corporate networks, and the Microsoft DHCP server, in particular, is a very popular option — <b>we saw the Microsoft DHCP server running on 40% of the data center networks we monitor.</b></p>
<p>Although modern Windows clients (<a href="https://www.itprotoday.com/compute-engines/dynamic-dns-updates-windows-2000" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Windows 2000 and above</a>) normally create their own records by sending DNS Dynamic Updates, this is not always the case. DNS records can also be created using a DHCP feature called <i>DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates</i>. <b>The purpose of this feature is to allow a DHCP server to register DNS records on behalf of its clients</b>. Whenever a client is given an IP address by the DHCP server, the latter can contact the DNS server and update the client’s DNS record. To perform these updates, the DHCP server uses (surprise, surprise!) DNS Dynamic Updates.</p>
<p>The name similarity can be quite confusing, so let's clarify:</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-7d6f6a505f&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:41:13Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;table>\r\n&lt;tbody>&lt;tr>&lt;th>&lt;p>Feature&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/th>\r\n&lt;th>&lt;p>Protocol&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/th>\r\n&lt;th>&lt;p>Purpose&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/th>\r\n&lt;/tr>&lt;tr>&lt;td>&lt;p>DNS Dynamic Updates&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/td>\r\n&lt;td>&lt;p>DNS&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/td>\r\n&lt;td>&lt;p>Allow DNS clients to create or modify DNS records on the DNS server&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/td>\r\n&lt;/tr>&lt;tr>&lt;td>&lt;p>DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/td>\r\n&lt;td>&lt;p>DHCP&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/td>\r\n&lt;td>&lt;p>Allow the DHCP server to create or modify DNS records on behalf of its clients — the updates are performed using DNS Dynamic Updates&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/td>\r\n&lt;/tr>&lt;/tbody>&lt;/table>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <table>
<tbody><tr><th><p>Feature</p>
</th>
<th><p>Protocol</p>
</th>
<th><p>Purpose</p>
</th>
</tr><tr><td><p>DNS Dynamic Updates</p>
</td>
<td><p>DNS</p>
</td>
<td><p>Allow DNS clients to create or modify DNS records on the DNS server</p>
</td>
</tr><tr><td><p>DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates</p>
</td>
<td><p>DHCP</p>
</td>
<td><p>Allow the DHCP server to create or modify DNS records on behalf of its clients — the updates are performed using DNS Dynamic Updates</p>
</td>
</tr></tbody></table>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

    <p>The DHCP DNS Dynamic Update process is shown in Figure 6.</p>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-f0f5b56b39&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:43:24Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The DHCP client gets an IP address from the DHCP server and informs it of its FQDN.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The DHCP server sends a Dynamic Update request to the DNS server.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The DNS server validates the request, creates a relevant record, and informs the DHCP server of the result in a Dynamic Update response.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>Note that even when the DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates feature is enabled, the default configuration requires that the client explicitly specifies that a DNS record should be created on their behalf (Figure 7).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <ol>
<li><p>The DHCP client gets an IP address from the DHCP server and informs it of its FQDN.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The DHCP server sends a Dynamic Update request to the DNS server.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The DNS server validates the request, creates a relevant record, and informs the DHCP server of the result in a Dynamic Update response.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Note that even when the DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates feature is enabled, the default configuration requires that the client explicitly specifies that a DNS record should be created on their behalf (Figure 7).</p>

<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-222c9e3e2c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:47:41Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>To specify this, the client needs to send a dedicated DHCP option.     <p>To specify this, the client needs to send a dedicated DHCP option. DHCP options (Figure 8) are additional fields that can be added to a DHCP packet, and are used by the client and server to exchange information.</p>

<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-6900b1ebc9&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T15:49:50Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>For our cause, the client sends the &lt;i>FQDN option&lt;/i>, specified in &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4702.html\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>RFC 4702&lt;/a>. This option allows the DHCP client to inform the server of its fully qualified domain name (FQDN) and specify whether it should register a DNS record on behalf of the client. To do this, the client can use the Server Flag, which is part of the FQDN option. Setting it to 1 tells the server that it should create a record based on the provided FQDN (Figure 9).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>For our cause, the client sends the <i>FQDN option</i>, specified in <a href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4702.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">RFC 4702</a>. This option allows the DHCP client to inform the server of its fully qualified domain name (FQDN) and specify whether it should register a DNS record on behalf of the client. To do this, the client can use the Server Flag, which is part of the FQDN option. Setting it to 1 tells the server that it should create a record based on the provided FQDN (Figure 9).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="container responsivegrid cmp-container--paddingLeftMedium cmp-container--paddingRightMedium aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

               <div class="image">

<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="After receiving this request, the DHCP server sends a DNS Dynamic Update and creates the requested record (Figure 10)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-f4bf0a9cbd&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:06:54Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;After receiving this request, the DHCP server sends a DNS Dynamic Update and creates the requested record (Figure 10).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;3815bba9-53ba-4752-8622-d15b938274b0&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:06:42Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-10.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-10.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="After receiving this request, the DHCP server sends a DNS Dynamic Update and creates the requested record (Figure 10)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 10: ADI DNS zone with our new record</span>
    
    
  </div>

<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b092c41bb2&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:06:17Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This feature is useful but is not commonly used today (as stated, most modern Windows clients simply create their own records). Despite that, &lt;b>Microsoft DHCP servers have this feature enabled by default, meaning the DHCP server will register a DNS record for any client that requests it.&lt;/b>&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Note that DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates &lt;b>require no authentication&lt;/b> by the DHCP client — anyone in the network can lease an IP from a DHCP server, so an attacker can essentially use the DHCP server to authenticate to the DNS server on behalf of themself. &lt;b>This grants the attacker access to the ADIDNS zone without any credentials.&lt;/b>&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Microsoft seems to be aware of the potential risks that this feature poses and acknowledges some of them, but this attack surface is still mostly unknown to attackers and defenders&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The following sections detail the attacks that could be performed by abusing DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This feature is useful but is not commonly used today (as stated, most modern Windows clients simply create their own records). Despite that, <b>Microsoft DHCP servers have this feature enabled by default, meaning the DHCP server will register a DNS record for any client that requests it.</b></p>
<p>Note that DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates <b>require no authentication</b> by the DHCP client — anyone in the network can lease an IP from a DHCP server, so an attacker can essentially use the DHCP server to authenticate to the DNS server on behalf of themself. <b>This grants the attacker access to the ADIDNS zone without any credentials.</b></p>
<p>Microsoft seems to be aware of the potential risks that this feature poses and acknowledges some of them, but this attack surface is still mostly unknown to attackers and defenders</p>
<p>The following sections detail the attacks that could be performed by abusing DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b33d7a8bc0&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:11:06Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>DHCP DNS spoofing&lt;/h2>\r\n&lt;p>&lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.netspi.com/blog/technical/network-penetration-testing/exploiting-adidns/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Previously described ADIDNS spoofing attacks&lt;/a> weaponized ADIDNS to improve the classic &lt;a href=\&#34;https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1557/001/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>LLMNR/NBNS spoofing&lt;/a> attack. After identifying unsuccessful name resolution attempts (“dead hosts”), an attacker would register the name in the ADI zone, making future name resolution attempts point to the attacker’s machine.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This attack could be very impactful, but it had one major prerequisite — valid domain credentials. &lt;b>By using the DHCP server we can bypass this requirement and operate without any credentials&lt;/b>. We can simply send a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update for any FQDN that doesn’t exist in the ADI zone, and the DHCP server will create it for us. We’re calling this variation of the attack &lt;i>DHCP DNS spoofing&lt;/i>. This technique was also &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.trustedsec.com/blog/injecting-rogue-dns-records-using-dhcp/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>covered in a blog post&lt;/a> by Hans Lakhan of TrustedSec.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>What DNS names could we use for this attack? Again drawing from &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.netspi.com/blog/technical/network-penetration-testing/adidns-revisited/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Robertson’s research&lt;/a>, some obvious candidates would not work.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The &lt;a href=\&#34;https://trelis24.github.io/2018/08/03/Windows-WPAD-Poisoning-Responder/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>notorious WPAD&lt;/a> hostname would be blocked by the &lt;a href=\&#34;https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/module/dnsserver/get-dnsserverglobalqueryblocklist?view=windowsserver2022-ps\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>global query block list&lt;/a> (GQBL) on the DNS server.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The wildcard record (“*”) can’t be created via dynamic DNS updates, so cannot be abused in this scenario.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;p>Without these two candidates, we are left with the option of identifying dead hosts that are specific to the network. We can identify them by sniffing the network for name resolution broadcasts over Link-Local Multicast Name Resolution (LLMNR) or NetBIOS Name Service (NBT-NS). After we identify a potential dead host, we can create a matching DNS record by sending a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update (Figure 11).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>DHCP DNS spoofing</h2>
<p><a href="https://www.netspi.com/blog/technical/network-penetration-testing/exploiting-adidns/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Previously described ADIDNS spoofing attacks</a> weaponized ADIDNS to improve the classic <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1557/001/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">LLMNR/NBNS spoofing</a> attack. After identifying unsuccessful name resolution attempts (“dead hosts”), an attacker would register the name in the ADI zone, making future name resolution attempts point to the attacker’s machine.</p>
<p>This attack could be very impactful, but it had one major prerequisite — valid domain credentials. <b>By using the DHCP server we can bypass this requirement and operate without any credentials</b>. We can simply send a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update for any FQDN that doesn’t exist in the ADI zone, and the DHCP server will create it for us. We’re calling this variation of the attack <i>DHCP DNS spoofing</i>. This technique was also <a href="https://www.trustedsec.com/blog/injecting-rogue-dns-records-using-dhcp/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">covered in a blog post</a> by Hans Lakhan of TrustedSec.</p>
<p>What DNS names could we use for this attack? Again drawing from <a href="https://www.netspi.com/blog/technical/network-penetration-testing/adidns-revisited/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Robertson’s research</a>, some obvious candidates would not work.</p>
<ul>
<li><p>The <a href="https://trelis24.github.io/2018/08/03/Windows-WPAD-Poisoning-Responder/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">notorious WPAD</a> hostname would be blocked by the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/module/dnsserver/get-dnsserverglobalqueryblocklist?view=windowsserver2022-ps" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">global query block list</a> (GQBL) on the DNS server.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The wildcard record (“*”) can’t be created via dynamic DNS updates, so cannot be abused in this scenario.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Without these two candidates, we are left with the option of identifying dead hosts that are specific to the network. We can identify them by sniffing the network for name resolution broadcasts over Link-Local Multicast Name Resolution (LLMNR) or NetBIOS Name Service (NBT-NS). After we identify a potential dead host, we can create a matching DNS record by sending a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update (Figure 11).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title=" After we identify a potential dead host, we can create a matching DNS record by sending a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update (Figure 11)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-b63dc74f2b&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:12:47Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;After we identify a potential dead host, we can create a matching DNS record by sending a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update (Figure 11).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;12db8f1e-34ee-4356-b37a-2d4ddcc79729&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T15:50:06Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig11.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig11.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt=" After we identify a potential dead host, we can create a matching DNS record by sending a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update (Figure 11)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 11: Using DHCP DNS Dynamic Update to spoof dead hostnames</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b2f99cce30&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:18:33Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>A host in the network attempts to resolve the name “PC.aka.test”, and sends the DNS server a query.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>“PC.aka.test” is unknown to the DNS server, so it responds with “No such name”.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The host then sends an LLMNR multicast to try and locate the “PC.aka.test” in its LAN.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The attacker identifies this attempt and requests an IP lease from the DHCP server with “PC.aka.test” as the FQDN.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>The server sends a Dynamic Update request to the DNS server and the record is created.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>Now, the next time that any host in the network tries to resolve “PC.aka.test”, they will be redirected to the attacker. All the attacker has to do now is to fire up &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/fortra/impacket/blob/master/examples/ntlmrelayx.py\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>ntlmrelayx.py&lt;/a> and wait for authentication attempts.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This approach is better than both the standard LLMNR/NBNS spoofing method and the ADIDNS spoofing variation.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Classic LLMNR/NBNS spoofing requires no authentication, but is limited to victims in the same LAN (as LLMNR/NBNS are broadcast-based).&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>ADIDNS spoofing allowed us to target victims outside the LAN (as DNS works across subnets), but required an authenticated user.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;p>With DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates we get the best of both worlds — the attack works on victims outside the LAN, and doesn’t require any authentication.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This is pretty cool, but we can do even better.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <ol>
<li><p>A host in the network attempts to resolve the name “PC.aka.test”, and sends the DNS server a query.</p>
</li>
<li><p>“PC.aka.test” is unknown to the DNS server, so it responds with “No such name”.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The host then sends an LLMNR multicast to try and locate the “PC.aka.test” in its LAN.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The attacker identifies this attempt and requests an IP lease from the DHCP server with “PC.aka.test” as the FQDN.</p>
</li>
<li><p>The server sends a Dynamic Update request to the DNS server and the record is created.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Now, the next time that any host in the network tries to resolve “PC.aka.test”, they will be redirected to the attacker. All the attacker has to do now is to fire up <a href="https://github.com/fortra/impacket/blob/master/examples/ntlmrelayx.py" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">ntlmrelayx.py</a> and wait for authentication attempts.</p>
<p>This approach is better than both the standard LLMNR/NBNS spoofing method and the ADIDNS spoofing variation. </p>
<ul>
<li><p>Classic LLMNR/NBNS spoofing requires no authentication, but is limited to victims in the same LAN (as LLMNR/NBNS are broadcast-based).</p>
</li>
<li><p>ADIDNS spoofing allowed us to target victims outside the LAN (as DNS works across subnets), but required an authenticated user.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>With DHCP DNS Dynamic Updates we get the best of both worlds — the attack works on victims outside the LAN, and doesn’t require any authentication.</p>
<p>This is pretty cool, but we can do even better.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-e1da17d120&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:19:22Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h2>Overwriting existing records&lt;/h2>\r\n&lt;p>Creating nonexisting DNS records is cool, but this led us to think about another option: What happens if we try to create a record for a hostname that already exists? Could we possibly overwrite them somehow? Ideally, it shouldn’t be possible — right? Well…&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>We identified cases where it could be possible for unauthenticated attackers to overwrite existing records. We’re calling this technique &lt;i>DHCP DNS overwrite&lt;/i>. Before we get into those cases, let&#39;s discuss some more details about the DHCP Dynamic Updates process.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h2>Overwriting existing records</h2>
<p>Creating nonexisting DNS records is cool, but this led us to think about another option: What happens if we try to create a record for a hostname that already exists? Could we possibly overwrite them somehow? Ideally, it shouldn’t be possible — right? Well…</p>
<p>We identified cases where it could be possible for unauthenticated attackers to overwrite existing records. We’re calling this technique <i>DHCP DNS overwrite</i>. Before we get into those cases, let's discuss some more details about the DHCP Dynamic Updates process.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-0c5c14f32c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:20:11Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>DNS record types and their owners&lt;/h3>\r\n&lt;p>In the context of DHCP DNS attacks, there&#39;s an important distinction to make between two types of DNS records (Figure 12). We will use the following terms from now on:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>&lt;b>Client records&lt;/b>: Records that were created by Windows clients directly&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>&lt;b>Managed records&lt;/b>: Records that were created by the DHCP server on behalf of clients&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>DNS record types and their owners</h3>
<p>In the context of DHCP DNS attacks, there's an important distinction to make between two types of DNS records (Figure 12). We will use the following terms from now on:</p>
<ul>
<li><p><b>Client records</b>: Records that were created by Windows clients directly</p>
</li>
<li><p><b>Managed records</b>: Records that were created by the DHCP server on behalf of clients</p>
</li>
</ul>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="In the context of DHCP DNS attacks, there&#39;s an important distinction to make between two types of DNS records (Figure 12)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-b24cf39405&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:14:46Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;In the context of DHCP DNS attacks, there&#39;s an important distinction to make between two types of DNS records (Figure 12).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;eb305d8d-320b-4487-bf94-abffa42d3898&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:07:31Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig12.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig12.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="In the context of DHCP DNS attacks, there&#39;s an important distinction to make between two types of DNS records (Figure 12)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 12: DNS record types</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-463b4dbafc&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T21:22:28Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>&lt;b>The crucial difference between these clients is their owner&lt;/b>. As we described &lt;a href=\&#34;#dns\&#34; target=\&#34;_self\&#34;>earlier in this post&lt;/a>, when a DNS update is performed, a client record is created, and the principal that sent the update request is assigned as the record owner. For normal Windows clients, this principal is the machine account of the client.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>One might expect that managed records would also be owned by their requesting client, but this is not the case. When the DHCP server sends DNS updates on behalf of clients, it also authenticates using &lt;b>its own&lt;/b> machine account — which becomes the record owner.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>We can see this difference in Figure 12. PC2 is a client record owned by the client, and PC1 is a managed record owned by the DHCP server.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p><b>The crucial difference between these clients is their owner</b>. As we described <a href="#dns" target="_self">earlier in this post</a>, when a DNS update is performed, a client record is created, and the principal that sent the update request is assigned as the record owner. For normal Windows clients, this principal is the machine account of the client.</p>
<p>One might expect that managed records would also be owned by their requesting client, but this is not the case. When the DHCP server sends DNS updates on behalf of clients, it also authenticates using <b>its own</b> machine account — which becomes the record owner.</p>
<p>We can see this difference in Figure 12. PC2 is a client record owned by the client, and PC1 is a managed record owned by the DHCP server.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b4e614deaf&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T21:11:07Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>Access control lists limit DHCP DNS overwrites&lt;/h3>\r\n&lt;p>When we try to perform a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update on an existing record — in this case, the “&lt;i>PC.aka.test&lt;/i>” record — we fail. An interesting behavior is observed: The DHCP server actually &lt;b>does send a DNS update with our supplied FQDN&lt;/b>, but the update is then refused by the server (Figure 13).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>Access control lists limit DHCP DNS overwrites</h3>
<p>When we try to perform a DHCP DNS Dynamic Update on an existing record — in this case, the “<i>PC.aka.test</i>” record — we fail. An interesting behavior is observed: The DHCP server actually <b>does send a DNS update with our supplied FQDN</b>, but the update is then refused by the server (Figure 13).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="An interesting behavior is observed: The DHCP server actually does send a DNS update with our supplied FQDN, but the update is then refused by the server (Figure 13)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-aa0456c921&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:23:40Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;An interesting behavior is observed: The DHCP server actually does send a DNS update with our supplied FQDN, but the update is then refused by the server (Figure 13).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;e6de8c10-53b1-4f8e-aa94-928bdca11f5f&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:23:32Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-13.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-13.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="An interesting behavior is observed: The DHCP server actually does send a DNS update with our supplied FQDN, but the update is then refused by the server (Figure 13)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 13: DHCP DNS update refused by the server</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-c27de24216&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T21:12:45Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This happens because the DHCP server is not authorized to modify the record.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>PC.aka.test is a &lt;b>client&lt;/b> record, which is owned by the &lt;i>PC$&lt;/i> principal. When the DHCP server sends the DNS update, it authenticates by using its own machine account — &lt;i>DHCP$&lt;/i>. Since this account doesn’t have permissions over the record, the update is refused (Figure 14).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This happens because the DHCP server is not authorized to modify the record.</p>
<p>PC.aka.test is a <b>client</b> record, which is owned by the <i>PC$</i> principal. When the DHCP server sends the DNS update, it authenticates by using its own machine account — <i>DHCP$</i>. Since this account doesn’t have permissions over the record, the update is refused (Figure 14).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Since this account doesn’t have permissions over the record, the update is refused (Figure 14)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-6ddb02834e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:16:00Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Since this account doesn’t have permissions over the record, the update is refused (Figure 14).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;9cc2d835-51c1-4ff4-b71a-eb1a2f051d54&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:15:52Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig14.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig14.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="Since this account doesn’t have permissions over the record, the update is refused (Figure 14)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 14: DNS record overwrite fails</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-72577ac3ad&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T16:26:13Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>To summarize: It is possible for attackers to use the DHCP server to send arbitrary DNS updates, but the DNS records should be safe from overwrites because of their ACLs.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Now that we understand the mechanism that’s supposed to prevent overwrites, let&#39;s see how they could still be performed.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>To summarize: It is possible for attackers to use the DHCP server to send arbitrary DNS updates, but the DNS records should be safe from overwrites because of their ACLs.</p>
<p>Now that we understand the mechanism that’s supposed to prevent overwrites, let's see how they could still be performed.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-e86c7471b5&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-06T21:12:58Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>Managed record overwrite&lt;/h3>\r\n&lt;p>Although overwrites of existing client records don’t work because of their restricting ACLs, overwrites of managed records (ones created by DHCP) &lt;i>do work&lt;/i>, as the authenticating machine is also the record owner (Figure 15).&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This is possible because a DHCP server doesn’t verify DNS record ownership and sends a DNS update for any requested FQDN.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>Managed record overwrite</h3>
<p>Although overwrites of existing client records don’t work because of their restricting ACLs, overwrites of managed records (ones created by DHCP) <i>do work</i>, as the authenticating machine is also the record owner (Figure 15).</p>
<p>This is possible because a DHCP server doesn’t verify DNS record ownership and sends a DNS update for any requested FQDN.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Although overwrites of existing client records don’t work because of their restricting ACLs, overwrites of managed records (ones created by DHCP) do work, as the authenticating machine is also the record owner (Figure 15)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-0e5951f4ce&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T17:04:37Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Although overwrites of existing client records don’t work because of their restricting ACLs, overwrites of managed records (ones created by DHCP) do work, as the authenticating machine is also the record owner (Figure 15).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;8fb49bb2-facc-4198-9061-89fb144632f1&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T17:03:22Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig15.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig15.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="Although overwrites of existing client records don’t work because of their restricting ACLs, overwrites of managed records (ones created by DHCP) do work, as the authenticating machine is also the record owner (Figure 15)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 15: DHCP DNS overwrite of records owned by the DHCP server</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-4823331451&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:00:15Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>As we can see, the DHCP server performs an update using the same account that owns the record — its own — and so the update succeeds.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Let&#39;s look at an example. We boot an Ubuntu server, which is not part of the domain and therefore cannot register its own DNS record. Instead, it asks the DHCP server to do it on its behalf (Figure 16).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>As we can see, the DHCP server performs an update using the same account that owns the record — its own — and so the update succeeds.</p>
<p>Let's look at an example. We boot an Ubuntu server, which is not part of the domain and therefore cannot register its own DNS record. Instead, it asks the DHCP server to do it on its behalf (Figure 16).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="It asks the DHCP server to do it on its behalf (Figure 16)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-032d804bf3&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:01:16Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;It asks the DHCP server to do it on its behalf (Figure 16).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;4d4c90ac-ad3c-4a5d-8744-cc7524fa7304&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:01:07Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-16.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-16.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="It asks the DHCP server to do it on its behalf (Figure 16)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 16: DHCP server registers a DNS record on behalf of the Ubuntu server</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-68ed1cec55&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:01:51Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This record is owned by the DHCP server machine account. Now, from our attacking machine we request the same FQDN from the DHCP server in the lease process. We check the DNS zone and see that our overwrite was successful, and the record now points to the IP that was just leased to us (Figure 17).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This record is owned by the DHCP server machine account. Now, from our attacking machine we request the same FQDN from the DHCP server in the lease process. We check the DNS zone and see that our overwrite was successful, and the record now points to the IP that was just leased to us (Figure 17).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="We check the DNS zone and see that our overwrite was successful, and the record now points to the IP that was just leased to us (Figure 17)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-540ebec9e4&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:03:08Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;We check the DNS zone and see that our overwrite was successful, and the record now points to the IP that was just leased to us (Figure 17).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;0cdc8858-d64b-41f5-b8e5-fc0b131b5836&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:03:00Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-17.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-17.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="We check the DNS zone and see that our overwrite was successful, and the record now points to the IP that was just leased to us (Figure 17)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 17: Overwritten Ubuntu server DNS record</span>
    
    
  </div>

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b75dff427c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:03:36Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This attack is okay, but its impact is pretty limited as it only affects managed records. As we’ve mentioned before, these records are much less common than client records, which are not affected by this attack. Despite that, managed records can still be found in some cases in which the client is unable to register their own record, such as:&lt;br>\r\n&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Non-Windows clients&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Legacy Windows clients&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Windows clients that disabled client DNS updates&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This attack is okay, but its impact is pretty limited as it only affects managed records. As we’ve mentioned before, these records are much less common than client records, which are not affected by this attack. Despite that, managed records can still be found in some cases in which the client is unable to register their own record, such as:<br />
</p>
<ul>
<li><p>Non-Windows clients</p>
</li>
<li><p>Legacy Windows clients </p>
</li>
<li><p>Windows clients that disabled client DNS updates</p>
</li>
</ul>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-fba18b33d5&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:39:27Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>DHCP self-overwrite&lt;/h3>\r\n&lt;p>To increase the potential impact, we want to be able to overwrite records that are present in any ADI zone — client records. The problem is that these records are owned by the machines that created them, and we can only authenticate using the DHCP server’s machine account.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>But &lt;b>what about the DNS record of the DHCP server?&lt;/b> When the DHCP server creates its own DNS record, its machine account becomes the record owner! It turns out that &lt;b>we can make the DHCP server perform DHCP DNS overwrite on itself.&lt;/b> If we supply the DHCP server name as our FQDN, the DHCP server will send a DNS update for its own client record — and this overwrite would succeed!&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>DHCP self-overwrite</h3>
<p>To increase the potential impact, we want to be able to overwrite records that are present in any ADI zone — client records. The problem is that these records are owned by the machines that created them, and we can only authenticate using the DHCP server’s machine account.</p>
<p>But <b>what about the DNS record of the DHCP server?</b> When the DHCP server creates its own DNS record, its machine account becomes the record owner! It turns out that <b>we can make the DHCP server perform DHCP DNS overwrite on itself.</b> If we supply the DHCP server name as our FQDN, the DHCP server will send a DNS update for its own client record — and this overwrite would succeed!</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="I used the DHCP to destroy the DHCP" class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-515aa3b2b3&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:41:14Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;I used the DHCP to destroy the DHCP&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;0cb4fecf-6ea3-422f-9c92-1a26f1fa8794&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:40:54Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-18.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-18.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="I used the DHCP to destroy the DHCP"/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Figure 18 shows this attack flow.</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="DHCP DNS overwrite" class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-bcac327fe8&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:18:19Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;DHCP DNS overwrite&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;cb45d75f-ba38-4295-af02-4d2d27a70464&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-07T16:18:11Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig18.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig18.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="DHCP DNS overwrite"/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 18: DHCP DNS overwrite of the DHCP server DNS record</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-1f2234603c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:43:08Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This attack is more reliable: If a Microsoft DHCP server is present in the network, a matching client record for it is guaranteed, whereas managed records (that are needed for the previous overwrite scenario) are more rare.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>As for the impact, attackers would be able to intercept any communication destined for the DHCP server. The severity would depend on the nature of this traffic. In most cases, the ability to intercept communication destined for the DHCP server could be abused to intercept credentials and relay them, or capture sensitive traffic of other services that might be installed on the server.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Speaking of sensitive services: What if the DHCP server is installed on a domain controller (DC)? Can we overwrite the DC record? Well, it turns out we can.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This attack is more reliable: If a Microsoft DHCP server is present in the network, a matching client record for it is guaranteed, whereas managed records (that are needed for the previous overwrite scenario) are more rare.</p>
<p>As for the impact, attackers would be able to intercept any communication destined for the DHCP server. The severity would depend on the nature of this traffic. In most cases, the ability to intercept communication destined for the DHCP server could be abused to intercept credentials and relay them, or capture sensitive traffic of other services that might be installed on the server.</p>
<p>Speaking of sensitive services: What if the DHCP server is installed on a domain controller (DC)? Can we overwrite the DC record? Well, it turns out we can.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-51726cbab5&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:47:04Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;h3>DHCP DC arbitrary overwrite&lt;/h3>\r\n&lt;p>If the DHCP server is installed on a DC, we can perform a DHCP DNS overwrite on the DC’s own record (because of the reasons we described earlier in this post). That can be very useful, but there’s more we can do.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>As we already know, if the DHCP server is installed on a DC, the DC’s machine account will be used when sending DNS updates. Interestingly, if we inspect the default ACL of an arbitrary DNS record, we will see that &lt;b>the &lt;i>ENTERPRISE DOMAIN CONTROLLERS&lt;/i> principal has write permission over every DNS record in the zone — regardless of who created it&lt;/b> (Figure 19).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <h3>DHCP DC arbitrary overwrite</h3>
<p>If the DHCP server is installed on a DC, we can perform a DHCP DNS overwrite on the DC’s own record (because of the reasons we described earlier in this post). That can be very useful, but there’s more we can do.</p>
<p>As we already know, if the DHCP server is installed on a DC, the DC’s machine account will be used when sending DNS updates. Interestingly, if we inspect the default ACL of an arbitrary DNS record, we will see that <b>the <i>ENTERPRISE DOMAIN CONTROLLERS</i> principal has write permission over every DNS record in the zone — regardless of who created it</b> (Figure 19).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="container responsivegrid cmp-container--paddingLeftMedium cmp-container--paddingRightMedium aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">

    
    
    
   

    

          <div data-scroll-to-id="container-18330b2ec3" class="cmp-container ">
          
               <div class="image">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Interestingly, if we inspect the default ACL of an arbitrary DNS record, we will see that the ENTERPRISE DOMAIN CONTROLLERS principal has write permission over every DNS record in the zone — regardless of who created it (Figure 19)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-027216e6e6&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:46:42Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Interestingly, if we inspect the default ACL of an arbitrary DNS record, we will see that the ENTERPRISE DOMAIN CONTROLLERS principal has write permission over every DNS record in the zone — regardless of who created it (Figure 19).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;542b351f-74b7-4072-b615-c0f8352cda59&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:46:33Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/png&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-19.png&#34;}}}">
    
      
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig-19.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="Interestingly, if we inspect the default ACL of an arbitrary DNS record, we will see that the ENTERPRISE DOMAIN CONTROLLERS principal has write permission over every DNS record in the zone — regardless of who created it (Figure 19)."/>
        
        
      
    
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 19: Default ACL of all domain records containing the ENTERPRISE DOMAIN CONTROLLERS group</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>

               
          </div>

</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-a408545c7b&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2023-12-05T17:47:48Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This is huge. If the DHCP server is a DC, then it has permissions over all the records in the zone, and attackers could use it to &lt;b>overwrite any DNS A record inside the ADI zone — as an unauthenticated user!&lt;/b> The attack is illustrated in Figure 20.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This is huge. If the DHCP server is a DC, then it has permissions over all the records in the zone, and attackers could use it to <b>overwrite any DNS A record inside the ADI zone — as an unauthenticated user!</b> The attack is illustrated in Figure 20.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper    ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="This is huge.           <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2023/spoofing-dns-by-abusing-dhcp-fig20.png" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="This is huge.     <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 20: Arbitrary DHCP DNS overwrite when the DHCP server is a DC</span>
    
    
  </div>
